	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Quasar</span></h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		<div id="contentSub2"></div>
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable"><span>For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Quasar_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Quasar (disambiguation)">Quasar (disambiguation)</a>.</span> <span>"Quasi-stellar object" redirects here. Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Quasi-star" title="Quasi-star">Quasi-star</a>.</span></div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Active galactic nucleus containing a supermassive black hole</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_rendering_ULAS_J1120%2B0641.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Artist%27s_rendering_ULAS_J1120%2B0641.jpg/300px-Artist%27s_rendering_ULAS_J1120%2B0641.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Artist%27s_rendering_ULAS_J1120%2B0641.jpg/450px-Artist%27s_rendering_ULAS_J1120%2B0641.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Artist%27s_rendering_ULAS_J1120%2B0641.jpg/600px-Artist%27s_rendering_ULAS_J1120%2B0641.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4112" data-file-height="2438" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_rendering_ULAS_J1120%2B0641.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's rendering of the accretion disc in <a href="/wiki/ULAS_J1120%2B0641" title="ULAS J1120+0641">ULAS J1120+0641</a>, a very distant quasar powered by a supermassive black hole with a mass two billion times that of the Sun<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>quasar</b> is an extremely <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminous</a> <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">active galactic nucleus</a> (AGN). It is pronounced <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/ɑːr/: &#39;ar&#39; in &#39;far&#39;">ɑːr</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">KWAY</span>-zar</i></a>, and sometimes known as a <b>quasi-stellar object</b>, abbreviated <b>QSO</b>. This emission from a <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_nucleus" class="mw-redirect" title="Galaxy nucleus">galaxy nucleus</a> is powered by a <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a> with a mass ranging from millions to tens of billions of <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a>, surrounded by a gaseous <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Accretion disc">accretion disc</a>. Gas in the disc falling towards the black hole heats up because of friction and releases <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiant energy</a> of quasars is enormous; the most powerful quasars have <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosities</a> thousands of times greater than that of a <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Usually, quasars are categorized as a subclass of the more general category of AGN. The <a href="/wiki/Redshifts" class="mw-redirect" title="Redshifts">redshifts</a> of quasars are of <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">cosmological origin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <em>quasar</em> originated as a <a href="/wiki/Contraction_(grammar)" title="Contraction (grammar)">contraction</a> of "quasi-stellar <i>[star-like]</i> radio source"—because quasars were first identified during the 1950s as sources of radio-wave emission of unknown physical origin—and when identified in photographic images at visible wavelengths, they resembled faint, star-like points of light. High-resolution images of quasars, particularly from the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>, have demonstrated that quasars occur in the <a href="/wiki/Central_massive_object" title="Central massive object">centers of galaxies</a>, and that some host galaxies are strongly <a href="/wiki/Interacting_galaxy" title="Interacting galaxy">interacting</a> or <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_merger" title="Galaxy merger">merging</a> galaxies.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> As with other categories of AGN, the observed properties of a quasar depend on many factors, including the mass of the black hole, the rate of gas accretion, the orientation of the accretion disc relative to the observer, the presence or absence of a <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_jet" title="Astrophysical jet">jet</a>, and the degree of <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(astronomy)" title="Extinction (astronomy)">obscuration</a> by gas and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a> within the host galaxy.
</p><p>More than a million quasars have been found,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> with the <a href="/wiki/Markarian_231" title="Markarian 231">nearest known</a> being about 600 million <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> away from Earth. The record for the most distant known quasar continues to change. In 2017, the quasar <a href="/wiki/ULAS_J1342%2B0928" title="ULAS J1342+0928">ULAS J1342+0928</a> was detected at <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> <i>z</i> = 7.54. Light observed from this 800-million-<a href="/wiki/Solar-mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar-mass">solar-mass</a> quasar was emitted when the universe was only 690&#160;million years old.<sup id="cite_ref-NAT-20171206_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20171206-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SPC-20171206_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPC-20171206-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20171206_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20171206-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 2020, the quasar <a href="/wiki/P%C5%8Dniu%C4%81%CA%BBena" title="Pōniuāʻena">Pōniuāʻena</a> was detected from a time only 700&#160;million years after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, and with an estimated mass of 1.5&#160;billion times the mass of the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In early 2021, the quasar <a href="/wiki/J0313%E2%80%931806" title="J0313–1806">J0313–1806</a>, with a 1.6-billion-solar-mass black hole, was reported at <i>z</i> = 7.64, 670&#160;million years after the Big Bang.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Quasar discovery surveys have demonstrated that quasar activity was more common in the distant past; the peak epoch was approximately 10&#160;billion years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Concentrations of multiple, gravitationally attracted quasars are known as <a href="/wiki/Large_quasar_group" title="Large quasar group">large quasar groups</a> and constitute some of the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">largest known structures</a> in the universe.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term "quasar" was first used in an article by <a href="/wiki/Astrophysicist" class="mw-redirect" title="Astrophysicist">astrophysicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Hong-Yee_Chiu" title="Hong-Yee Chiu">Hong-Yee Chiu</a> in May 1964, in <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_Today" title="Physics Today">Physics Today</a></i>, to describe certain astronomically puzzling objects:<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>So far, the clumsily long name "quasi-stellar radio sources" is used to describe these objects. Because the nature of these objects is entirely unknown, it is hard to prepare a short, appropriate nomenclature for them so that their essential properties are obvious from their name. For convenience, the abbreviated form "quasar" will be used throughout this paper.</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_observation_and_interpretation">History of observation and interpretation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History of observation and interpretation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SDSS_image_of_quasar_3C273.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/SDSS_image_of_quasar_3C273.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SDSS_image_of_quasar_3C273.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey" title="Sloan Digital Sky Survey">Sloan Digital Sky Survey</a> image of quasar <a href="/wiki/3C_273" title="3C 273">3C 273</a>, illustrating the object's star-like appearance. The quasar's jet can be seen extending downward and to the right from the quasar.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quasar_viewed_from_Hubble.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Quasar_viewed_from_Hubble.jpg/200px-Quasar_viewed_from_Hubble.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Quasar_viewed_from_Hubble.jpg/300px-Quasar_viewed_from_Hubble.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Quasar_viewed_from_Hubble.jpg/400px-Quasar_viewed_from_Hubble.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2861" data-file-height="1737" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quasar_viewed_from_Hubble.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a> images of quasar <a href="/wiki/3C_273" title="3C 273">3C 273</a>. At right, a <a href="/wiki/Coronagraph" title="Coronagraph">coronagraph</a> is used to block the quasar's light, making it easier to detect the surrounding host galaxy.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy#Distinction_from_other_nebulae" title="Galaxy">Galaxy §&#160;Distinction from other nebulae</a></div>
<p>Between 1917 and 1922, it became clear from work by <a href="/wiki/Heber_Curtis" class="mw-redirect" title="Heber Curtis">Heber Curtis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_%C3%96pik" title="Ernst Öpik">Ernst Öpik</a> and others that some objects ("<a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a>") seen by astronomers were in fact distant <a href="/wiki/Galaxies" class="mw-redirect" title="Galaxies">galaxies</a> like the Milky Way. But when <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio astronomy</a> began in the 1950s, astronomers detected, among the galaxies, a small number of anomalous objects with properties that defied explanation.
</p><p>The objects emitted large amounts of radiation of many frequencies, but no source could be located optically, or in some cases only a faint and <a href="/wiki/Point-like" class="mw-redirect" title="Point-like">point-like</a> object somewhat like a distant <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> of these objects, which identify the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> of which the object is composed, were also extremely strange and defied explanation. Some of them changed their <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a> very rapidly in the optical range and even more rapidly in the X-ray range, suggesting an upper limit on their size, perhaps no larger than the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> This implies an extremely high <a href="/wiki/Power_density" title="Power density">power density</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Considerable discussion took place over what these objects might be. They were described as <i>"quasi-stellar</i> [meaning: star-like] <i>radio sources"</i>, or <i>"quasi-stellar objects"</i> (QSOs), a name which reflected their unknown nature, and this became shortened to "quasar".
</p>
<h3><span id="Early_observations_.281960s_and_earlier.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_observations_(1960s_and_earlier)">Early observations (1960s and earlier)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early observations (1960s and earlier)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first quasars (<a href="/wiki/3C_48" title="3C 48">3C 48</a> and <a href="/wiki/3C_273" title="3C 273">3C 273</a>) were discovered in the late 1950s, as radio sources in all-sky radio surveys.<sup id="cite_ref-Shields_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shields-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> They were first noted as radio sources with no corresponding visible object. Using small telescopes and the <a href="/wiki/Lovell_Telescope" title="Lovell Telescope">Lovell Telescope</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Interferometry" title="Interferometry">interferometer</a>, they were shown to have a very small angular size.<sup id="cite_ref-jbo_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbo-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> By 1960, hundreds of these objects had been recorded and published in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Cambridge_Catalogue" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Cambridge Catalogue">Third Cambridge Catalogue</a> while astronomers scanned the skies for their optical counterparts. In 1963, a definite identification of the radio source <a href="/wiki/3C_48" title="3C 48">3C 48</a> with an optical object was published by <a href="/wiki/Allan_Sandage" title="Allan Sandage">Allan Sandage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Matthews" title="Thomas A. Matthews">Thomas A. Matthews</a>. Astronomers had detected what appeared to be a faint blue star at the location of the radio source and obtained its spectrum, which contained many unknown broad emission lines. The anomalous spectrum defied interpretation.
</p><p>British-Australian astronomer <a href="/wiki/John_Gatenby_Bolton" title="John Gatenby Bolton">John Bolton</a> made many early observations of quasars, including a breakthrough in 1962. Another radio source, <a href="/wiki/3C_273" title="3C 273">3C 273</a>, was predicted to undergo five <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultations</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Measurements taken by <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Hazard" title="Cyril Hazard">Cyril Hazard</a> and John Bolton during one of the occultations using the <a href="/wiki/Parkes_Radio_Telescope" class="mw-redirect" title="Parkes Radio Telescope">Parkes Radio Telescope</a> allowed <a href="/wiki/Maarten_Schmidt" title="Maarten Schmidt">Maarten Schmidt</a> to find a visible counterpart to the radio source and obtain an <a href="/wiki/Optical_spectrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical spectrum">optical spectrum</a> using the 200-inch (5.1&#160;m) <a href="/wiki/Hale_Telescope" title="Hale Telescope">Hale Telescope</a> on <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Mountain" title="Palomar Mountain">Mount Palomar</a>. This spectrum revealed the same strange emission lines. Schmidt was able to demonstrate that these were likely to be the ordinary <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> of hydrogen redshifted by 15.8%, at the time, a high redshift (with only a handful of much fainter galaxies known with higher redshift). If this was due to the physical motion of the "star", then 3C 273 was receding at an enormous velocity, around <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7007470000000000000♠"></span>47<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span>&#160;km/s</span>, far beyond the speed of any known star and defying any obvious explanation.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Nor would an extreme velocity help to explain 3C 273's huge radio emissions. If the redshift was cosmological (now known to be correct), the large distance implied that 3C 273 was far more luminous than any galaxy, but much more compact. Also, 3C 273 was bright enough to detect on archival photographs dating back to the 1900s; it was found to be variable on yearly timescales, implying that a substantial fraction of the light was emitted from a region less than 1&#160;light-year in size, tiny compared to a galaxy.
</p><p>Although it raised many questions, Schmidt's discovery quickly revolutionized quasar observation. The strange spectrum of <a href="/wiki/3C_48" title="3C 48">3C 48</a> was quickly identified by Schmidt, Greenstein and Oke as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> redshifted by 37%. Shortly afterwards, two more quasar spectra in 1964 and five more in 1965 were also confirmed as ordinary light that had been redshifted to an extreme degree.<sup id="cite_ref-Caltech_p3_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caltech_p3-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> While the observations and redshifts themselves were not doubted, their correct interpretation was heavily debated, and Bolton's suggestion that the radiation detected from quasars were ordinary <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> from distant highly redshifted sources with extreme velocity was not widely accepted at the time.
</p>
<h3><span id="Development_of_physical_understanding_.281960s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_physical_understanding_(1960s)">Development of physical understanding (1960s)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Development of physical understanding (1960s)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">Redshift</a>, <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">Expansion of the universe</a></div>
<p>An extreme redshift could imply great distance and velocity but could also be due to extreme mass or perhaps some other unknown laws of nature. Extreme velocity and distance would also imply immense power output, which lacked explanation. The small sizes were confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Interferometry" title="Interferometry">interferometry</a> and by observing the speed with which the quasar as a whole varied in output, and by their inability to be seen in even the most powerful visible-light telescopes as anything more than faint starlike points of light. But if they were small and far away in space, their power output would have to be immense and difficult to explain. Equally, if they were very small and much closer to this galaxy, it would be easy to explain their apparent power output, but less easy to explain their redshifts and lack of detectable movement against the background of the universe.
</p><p>Schmidt noted that redshift is also associated with the expansion of the universe, as codified in <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble&#39;s law">Hubble's law</a>. If the measured redshift was due to expansion, then this would support an interpretation of very distant objects with extraordinarily high <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a> and power output, far beyond any object seen to date. This extreme luminosity would also explain the large radio signal. Schmidt concluded that 3C 273 could either be an individual star around 10&#160;km wide within (or near to) this galaxy, or a distant active galactic nucleus. He stated that a distant and extremely powerful object seemed more likely to be correct.<sup id="cite_ref-schmidt1963_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmidt1963-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Schmidt's explanation for the high redshift was not widely accepted at the time. A major concern was the enormous amount of energy these objects would have to be radiating, if they were distant. In the 1960s no commonly accepted mechanism could account for this. The currently accepted explanation, that it is due to <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Accretion disc">accretion disc</a> falling into a <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a>, was only suggested in 1964 by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Ernest_Salpeter" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin Ernest Salpeter">Edwin Salpeter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yakov_Zeldovich" title="Yakov Zeldovich">Yakov Zeldovich</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and even then it was rejected by many astronomers, as at this time the existence of <a href="/wiki/Black_holes" class="mw-redirect" title="Black holes">black holes</a> at all was widely seen as theoretical.
</p><p>Various explanations were proposed during the 1960s and 1970s, each with their own problems. It was suggested that quasars were nearby objects, and that their redshift was not due to the <a href="/wiki/Redshift#Expansion_of_space" title="Redshift">expansion of space</a> but rather to <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_redshift" title="Gravitational redshift">light escaping a deep gravitational well</a>. This would require a massive object, which would also explain the high luminosities. However, a star of sufficient mass to produce the measured redshift would be unstable and in excess of the <a href="/wiki/Hayashi_limit" title="Hayashi limit">Hayashi limit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Quasars also show <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_lines" class="mw-redirect" title="Forbidden lines">forbidden</a> spectral emission lines, previously only seen in hot gaseous nebulae of low density, which would be too diffuse to both generate the observed power and fit within a deep gravitational well.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> There were also serious concerns regarding the idea of cosmologically distant quasars. One strong argument against them was that they implied energies that were far in excess of known energy conversion processes, including <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. There were suggestions that quasars were made of some hitherto unknown stable form of <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> in similarly unknown types of region of space, and that this might account for their brightness.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Others speculated that quasars were a <a href="/wiki/White_hole" title="White hole">white hole</a> end of a <a href="/wiki/Wormhole" title="Wormhole">wormhole</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> or a <a href="/wiki/Chain_reaction" title="Chain reaction">chain reaction</a> of numerous <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Caltech_ipac_4_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caltech_ipac_4-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eventually, starting from about the 1970s, many lines of evidence (including <a href="/wiki/Uhuru_(satellite)" title="Uhuru (satellite)">the first</a> <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_observatory" class="mw-redirect" title="Space observatory">space observatories</a>, knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a> and modern models of <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>) gradually demonstrated that the quasar redshifts are genuine and due to the <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Expansion of space">expansion of space</a>, that quasars are in fact as powerful and as distant as Schmidt and some other astronomers had suggested, and that their energy source is matter from an accretion disc falling onto a supermassive black hole.<sup id="cite_ref-keel2009_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keel2009-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This included crucial evidence from optical and X-ray viewing of quasar host galaxies, finding of "intervening" absorption lines, which explained various spectral anomalies, observations from <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Gunn_(astronomer)" title="James Gunn (astronomer)">Gunn</a>'s 1971 finding that galaxies containing quasars showed the same redshift as the quasars,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Kristian" title="Jerome Kristian">Kristian</a>'s 1973 finding that the "fuzzy" surrounding of many quasars was consistent with a less luminous host galaxy.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This model also fits well with other observations suggesting that many or even most galaxies have a massive central black hole. It would also explain why quasars are more common in the early universe: as a quasar draws matter from its accretion disc, there comes a point when there is less matter nearby, and energy production falls off or ceases, as the quasar becomes a more ordinary type of galaxy.
</p><p>The accretion-disc energy-production mechanism was finally modeled in the 1970s, and black holes were also directly detected (including evidence showing that supermassive black holes could be found at the centers of this and many other galaxies), which resolved the concern that quasars were too luminous to be a result of very distant objects or that a suitable mechanism could not be confirmed to exist in nature. By 1987 it was "well accepted" that this was the correct explanation for quasars,<sup id="cite_ref-thomsen_1987_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomsen_1987-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and the cosmological distance and energy output of quasars was accepted by almost all researchers.
</p>
<h3><span id="Modern_observations_.281970s_onward.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_observations_(1970s_onward)">Modern observations (1970s onward)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern observations (1970s onward)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UZC_J224030.2%2B032131.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/UZC_J224030.2%2B032131.jpg/220px-UZC_J224030.2%2B032131.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/UZC_J224030.2%2B032131.jpg/330px-UZC_J224030.2%2B032131.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/UZC_J224030.2%2B032131.jpg/440px-UZC_J224030.2%2B032131.jpg 2x" data-file-width="498" data-file-height="498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UZC_J224030.2%2B032131.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cosmic mirage known as the <a href="/wiki/Einstein_Cross" title="Einstein Cross">Einstein Cross</a>. Four apparent images are actually from the same quasar.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MUSE_spies_accreting_giant_structure_around_a_quasar.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/MUSE_spies_accreting_giant_structure_around_a_quasar.tif/lossy-page1-220px-MUSE_spies_accreting_giant_structure_around_a_quasar.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/MUSE_spies_accreting_giant_structure_around_a_quasar.tif/lossy-page1-330px-MUSE_spies_accreting_giant_structure_around_a_quasar.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/MUSE_spies_accreting_giant_structure_around_a_quasar.tif/lossy-page1-440px-MUSE_spies_accreting_giant_structure_around_a_quasar.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="614" data-file-height="656" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MUSE_spies_accreting_giant_structure_around_a_quasar.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cloud of gas around the distant quasar SDSS J102009.99+104002.7, taken by <a href="/wiki/Multi-unit_spectroscopic_explorer" title="Multi-unit spectroscopic explorer">MUSE</a><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Later it was found that not all quasars have strong radio emission; in fact only about 10% are "radio-loud". Hence the name "QSO" (quasi-stellar object) is used (in addition to "quasar") to refer to these objects, further categorised into the "radio-loud" and the "radio-quiet" classes. The discovery of the quasar had large implications for the field of astronomy in the 1960s, including drawing physics and astronomy closer together.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1979 the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">gravitational lens</a> effect predicted by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/General_theory_of_relativity" class="mw-redirect" title="General theory of relativity">general theory of relativity</a> was confirmed observationally for the first time with images of the <a href="/wiki/Twin_Quasar" title="Twin Quasar">double quasar</a> 0957+561.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study published in February 2021 showed that there are more quasars in one direction (towards <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(constellation)" title="Hydra (constellation)">Hydra</a>) than in the opposite direction, seemingly indicating that the Earth is moving in that direction. But the direction of this dipole is about 28° away from the direction of the Earth's motion relative to the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2021, a collaboration of scientists, related to the <a href="/wiki/Event_Horizon_Telescope" title="Event Horizon Telescope">Event Horizon Telescope</a>, presented, for the first time, a <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarized-based image</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>, particularly the black hole at the center of <a href="/wiki/Messier_87" title="Messier 87">Messier 87</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy" title="Elliptical galaxy">elliptical galaxy</a> approximately 55 million light-years away in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Virgo_(constellation)" title="Virgo (constellation)">Virgo</a>, revealing the forces giving rise to quasars.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20210324_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20210324-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_understanding">Current understanding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Current understanding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It is now known that quasars are distant but extremely luminous objects, so any light that reaches the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> is redshifted due to the <a href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric expansion of space">metric expansion of space</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-grupen_cowan2005_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grupen_cowan2005-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Quasars inhabit the centers of active galaxies and are among the most luminous, powerful, and energetic objects known in the universe, emitting up to a thousand times the energy output of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, which contains 200–400&#160;billion stars. This radiation is emitted across the electromagnetic spectrum, almost uniformly, from X-rays to the far infrared with a peak in the ultraviolet optical bands, with some quasars also being strong sources of radio emission and of gamma-rays. With high-resolution imaging from ground-based telescopes and the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>, the "host galaxies" surrounding the quasars have been detected in some cases.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> These galaxies are normally too dim to be seen against the glare of the quasar, except with special techniques. Most quasars, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/3C_273" title="3C 273">3C 273</a>, whose average <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> is 12.9, cannot be seen with small telescopes.
</p><p>Quasars are believed—and in many cases confirmed—to be powered by <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> of material into supermassive black holes in the nuclei of distant galaxies, as suggested in 1964 by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Salpeter" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin Salpeter">Edwin Salpeter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yakov_Zeldovich" title="Yakov Zeldovich">Yakov Zeldovich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shields_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shields-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Light and other radiation cannot escape from within the <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">event horizon</a> of a black hole. The energy produced by a quasar is generated <i>outside</i> the black hole, by gravitational stresses and immense <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> within the material nearest to the black hole, as it orbits and falls inward.<sup id="cite_ref-thomsen_1987_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomsen_1987-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The huge luminosity of quasars results from the accretion discs of central supermassive black holes, which can convert between 6% and 32% of the <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of an object into <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Lambourne_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lambourne-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> compared to just 0.7% for the <a href="/wiki/Proton%E2%80%93proton_chain" title="Proton–proton chain">p–p chain</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> process that dominates the energy production in Sun-like stars. Central masses of 10<sup>5</sup> to 10<sup>9</sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> have been measured in quasars by using <a href="/wiki/Reverberation_mapping" title="Reverberation mapping">reverberation mapping</a>. Several dozen nearby large galaxies, including the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy, that do not have an active center and do not show any activity similar to a quasar, are confirmed to contain a similar supermassive black hole in their <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">nuclei (galactic center)</a>. Thus it is now thought that all large galaxies have a black hole of this kind, but only a small fraction have sufficient matter in the right kind of orbit at their center to become active and power radiation in such a way as to be seen as quasars.<sup id="cite_ref-DiMatteo2005_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiMatteo2005-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This also explains why quasars were more common in the early universe, as this energy production ends when the supermassive black hole consumes all of the gas and dust near it. This means that it is possible that most galaxies, including the Milky Way, have gone through an active stage, appearing as a quasar or some other class of active galaxy that depended on the black-hole mass and the accretion rate, and are now quiescent because they lack a supply of matter to feed into their central black holes to generate radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-DiMatteo2005_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiMatteo2005-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quasars_in_interacting_galaxies.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Quasars_in_interacting_galaxies.jpg/220px-Quasars_in_interacting_galaxies.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Quasars_in_interacting_galaxies.jpg/330px-Quasars_in_interacting_galaxies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Quasars_in_interacting_galaxies.jpg/440px-Quasars_in_interacting_galaxies.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quasars_in_interacting_galaxies.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Quasars in interacting galaxies<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The matter accreting onto the black hole is unlikely to fall directly in, but will have some angular momentum around the black hole, which will cause the matter to collect into an <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Accretion disc">accretion disc</a>. Quasars may also be ignited or re-ignited when normal galaxies merge and the black hole is infused with a fresh source of matter. In fact, it has been suggested that a quasar could form when the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a> collides with the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy in approximately 3–5&#160;billion years.<sup id="cite_ref-thomsen_1987_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomsen_1987-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1980s, unified models were developed in which quasars were classified as a particular kind of <a href="/wiki/Active_galaxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Active galaxy">active galaxy</a>, and a consensus emerged that in many cases it is simply the viewing angle that distinguishes them from other active galaxies, such as <a href="/wiki/Blazar" title="Blazar">blazars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radio_galaxy" title="Radio galaxy">radio galaxies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The highest-redshift quasar known (as of December&#160;2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>) was <a href="/wiki/ULAS_J1342%2B0928" title="ULAS J1342+0928">ULAS J1342+0928</a>, with a redshift of 7.54,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> which corresponds to a <a href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" class="mw-redirect" title="Comoving distance">comoving distance</a> of approximately 29.36&#160;billion <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> from Earth (these distances are much larger than the distance light could travel in the universe's 13.8-billion-year history because <a href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space#Measuring_distances_in_expanding_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric expansion of space">space itself has also been expanding</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bright_halos_around_distant_quasars.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Bright_halos_around_distant_quasars.jpg/220px-Bright_halos_around_distant_quasars.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Bright_halos_around_distant_quasars.jpg/330px-Bright_halos_around_distant_quasars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Bright_halos_around_distant_quasars.jpg/440px-Bright_halos_around_distant_quasars.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bright_halos_around_distant_quasars.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bright halos around 18 distant quasars<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PKS_1127-145_X-rays.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/PKS_1127-145_X-rays.jpg/220px-PKS_1127-145_X-rays.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/PKS_1127-145_X-rays.jpg/330px-PKS_1127-145_X-rays.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/PKS_1127-145_X-rays.jpg 2x" data-file-width="420" data-file-height="420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PKS_1127-145_X-rays.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra</a> X-ray image is of the quasar PKS 1127-145, a highly luminous source of X-rays and visible light about 10 billion light-years from Earth. An enormous X-ray jet extends at least a million light-years from the quasar. Image is 60 arcseconds on a side. <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">RA</a> 11h 30m 7.10s <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">Dec</a> −14° 49' 27" in Crater. Observation date: May 28, 2000. Instrument: ACIS</div></div></div>
<p>More than <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005750414000000000♠"></span>750,414</span> quasars have been found (as of August 2020),<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> most from the <a href="/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey" title="Sloan Digital Sky Survey">Sloan Digital Sky Survey</a>. All observed quasar spectra have redshifts between 0.056 and 7.64 (as of 2021). Applying <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble&#39;s law">Hubble's law</a> to these redshifts, it can be shown that they are between 600 million<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and 29.36&#160;billion light-years away (in terms of <a href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" class="mw-redirect" title="Comoving distance">comoving distance</a>). Because of the great distances to the farthest quasars and the finite velocity of light, they and their surrounding space appear as they existed in the very early universe.
</p><p>The power of quasars originates from supermassive black holes that are believed to exist at the core of most galaxies. The Doppler shifts of stars near the cores of galaxies indicate that they are revolving around tremendous masses with very steep gravity gradients, suggesting black holes.
</p><p>Although quasars appear faint when viewed from Earth, they are visible from extreme distances, being the most luminous objects in the known universe. The brightest quasar in the sky is <a href="/wiki/3C_273" title="3C 273">3C 273</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Virgo_(constellation)" title="Virgo (constellation)">Virgo</a>. It has an average <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of 12.8 (bright enough to be seen through a medium-size amateur <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>), but it has an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of −26.7.<sup id="cite_ref-3C_273_article_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3C_273_article-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> From a distance of about 33&#160;light-years, this object would shine in the sky about as brightly as the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. This quasar's <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a> is, therefore, about 4&#160;trillion (4<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001120000000000000♠"></span>12</span></sup>) times that of the Sun, or about 100 times that of the total light of giant galaxies like the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3C_273_article_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3C_273_article-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> This assumes that the quasar is radiating energy in all directions, but the active galactic nucleus is believed to be radiating preferentially in the direction of its jet. In a universe containing hundreds of billions of galaxies, most of which had active nuclei billions of years ago but only seen today, it is statistically certain that thousands of energy jets should be pointed toward the Earth, some more directly than others. In many cases it is likely that the brighter the quasar, the more directly its jet is aimed at the Earth. Such quasars are called <a href="/wiki/Blazars" class="mw-redirect" title="Blazars">blazars</a>.
</p><p>The hyperluminous quasar <a href="/wiki/APM_08279%2B5255" title="APM 08279+5255">APM&#160;08279+5255</a> was, when discovered in 1998, given an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of −32.2. High-resolution imaging with the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> and the 10&#160;m <a href="/wiki/Keck_Telescope" class="mw-redirect" title="Keck Telescope">Keck Telescope</a> revealed that this system is <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitationally lensed</a>. A study of the gravitational lensing of this system suggests that the light emitted has been magnified by a factor of ~10. It is still substantially more luminous than nearby quasars such as 3C 273.
</p><p>Quasars were much more common in the early universe than they are today. This discovery by <a href="/wiki/Maarten_Schmidt" title="Maarten Schmidt">Maarten Schmidt</a> in 1967 was early strong evidence against <a href="/wiki/Steady-state_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Steady-state theory">steady-state cosmology</a> and in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> cosmology. Quasars show the locations where supermassive black holes are growing rapidly (by <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a>). Detailed simulations reported in 2021 showed that galaxy structures, such as spiral arms, use gravitational forces to 'put the brakes on' gas that would otherwise orbit galaxy centers forever; instead the braking mechanism enabled the gas to fall into the supermassive black holes, releasing enormous radiant energies.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> These black holes co-evolve with the mass of stars in their host galaxy in a way not fully understood at present. One idea is that jets, radiation and winds created by the quasars shut down the formation of new stars in the host galaxy, a process called "feedback". The jets that produce strong radio emission in some quasars at the centers of <a href="/wiki/Clusters_of_galaxies" class="mw-redirect" title="Clusters of galaxies">clusters of galaxies</a> are known to have enough power to prevent the hot gas in those clusters from cooling and falling on to the central galaxy.
</p><p>Quasars' luminosities are variable, with time scales that range from months to hours. This means that quasars generate and emit their energy from a very small region, since each part of the quasar would have to be in contact with other parts on such a time scale as to allow the coordination of the luminosity variations. This would mean that a quasar varying on a time scale of a few weeks cannot be larger than a few light-weeks across. The emission of large amounts of power from a small region requires a power source far more efficient than the nuclear fusion that powers stars. The conversion of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitational potential energy">gravitational potential energy</a> to radiation by infalling to a black hole converts between 6% and 32% of the mass to energy, compared to 0.7% for the conversion of mass to energy in a star like the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-Lambourne_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lambourne-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> It is the only process known that can produce such high power over a very long term. (Stellar explosions such as <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray bursts</a>, and direct <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>–<a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> annihilation, can also produce very high power output, but supernovae only last for days, and the universe does not appear to have had large amounts of antimatter at the relevant times.)
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quasar_HE_1104-1805.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Quasar_HE_1104-1805.jpg/220px-Quasar_HE_1104-1805.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Quasar_HE_1104-1805.jpg/330px-Quasar_HE_1104-1805.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Quasar_HE_1104-1805.jpg/440px-Quasar_HE_1104-1805.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quasar_HE_1104-1805.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gravitationally lensed quasar HE 1104-1805<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>

<p>Since quasars exhibit all the properties common to other <a href="/wiki/Active_galaxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Active galaxy">active galaxies</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Seyfert_galaxy" title="Seyfert galaxy">Seyfert galaxies</a>, the emission from quasars can be readily compared to those of smaller active galaxies powered by smaller supermassive black holes. To create a luminosity of 10<sup>40</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> (the typical brightness of a quasar), a supermassive black hole would have to consume the material equivalent of 10 solar masses per year. The brightest known quasars devour 1000 solar masses of material every year. The largest known is estimated to consume matter equivalent to 10 Earths per second. Quasar luminosities can vary considerably over time, depending on their surroundings. Since it is difficult to fuel quasars for many billions of years, after a quasar finishes accreting the surrounding gas and dust, it becomes an ordinary galaxy.
</p><p>Radiation from quasars is partially "nonthermal" (i.e., not due to <a href="/wiki/Black-body_radiation" title="Black-body radiation">black-body radiation</a>), and approximately 10% are observed to also have jets and lobes like those of <a href="/wiki/Radio_galaxy" title="Radio galaxy">radio galaxies</a> that also carry significant (but poorly understood) amounts of energy in the form of particles moving at <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_speed" title="Relativistic speed">relativistic speeds</a>. Extremely high energies might be explained by several mechanisms (see <a href="/wiki/Fermi_acceleration" title="Fermi acceleration">Fermi acceleration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_mechanism_of_acceleration" title="Centrifugal mechanism of acceleration">Centrifugal mechanism of acceleration</a>). Quasars can be detected over the entire observable <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Radio_waves" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio waves">radio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visible_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Visible light">visible light</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>. Most quasars are brightest in their rest-frame ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of 121.6&#160;<a href="/wiki/Nanometer" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanometer">nm</a> <a href="/wiki/Lyman_series" title="Lyman series">Lyman-alpha</a> emission line of hydrogen, but due to the tremendous redshifts of these sources, that peak luminosity has been observed as far to the red as 900.0&#160;nm, in the near infrared. A minority of quasars show strong radio emission, which is generated by jets of matter moving close to the speed of light. When viewed downward, these appear as <a href="/wiki/Blazar" title="Blazar">blazars</a> and often have regions that seem to move away from the center faster than the speed of light (<a href="/wiki/Superluminal" class="mw-redirect" title="Superluminal">superluminal</a> expansion). This is an optical illusion due to the properties of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>.
</p><p>Quasar redshifts are measured from the strong <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> that dominate their visible and ultraviolet emission spectra. These lines are brighter than the continuous spectrum. They exhibit <a href="/wiki/Doppler_broadening" title="Doppler broadening">Doppler broadening</a> corresponding to mean speed of several percent of the speed of light. Fast motions strongly indicate a large mass. Emission lines of hydrogen (mainly of the <a href="/wiki/Lyman_series" title="Lyman series">Lyman series</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balmer_series" title="Balmer series">Balmer series</a>), helium, carbon, magnesium, iron and oxygen are the brightest lines. The atoms emitting these lines range from neutral to highly ionized, leaving it highly charged. This wide range of ionization shows that the gas is highly irradiated by the quasar, not merely hot, and not by stars, which cannot produce such a wide range of ionization.
</p><p>Like all (unobscured) active galaxies, quasars can be strong X-ray sources. Radio-loud quasars can also produce X-rays and gamma rays by <a href="/wiki/Inverse_Compton_scattering" class="mw-redirect" title="Inverse Compton scattering">inverse Compton scattering</a> of lower-energy photons by the radio-emitting electrons in the jet.<sup id="cite_ref-Dooling_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dooling-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="iron_quasar"></span><i>Iron quasars</i> show strong emission lines resulting from low-ionization <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> (Fe&#160;<abbr title="singly ionized">II</abbr>), such as IRAS&#160;18508-7815.
</p>
<h3><span id="Spectral_lines.2C_reionization.2C_and_the_early_universe"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectral_lines,_reionization,_and_the_early_universe">Spectral lines, reionization, and the early universe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Spectral lines, reionization, and the early universe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Reionization" title="Reionization">Reionization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_Universe" class="mw-redirect" title="Chronology of the Universe">Chronology of the Universe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:QuasarStarburst.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/QuasarStarburst.jpg/220px-QuasarStarburst.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/QuasarStarburst.jpg/330px-QuasarStarburst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/QuasarStarburst.jpg/440px-QuasarStarburst.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:QuasarStarburst.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This view, taken with infrared light, is a false-color image of a quasar-starburst tandem with the most luminous <a href="/wiki/Starburst_(astronomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Starburst (astronomy)">starburst</a> ever seen in such a combination.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fingerprint_of_the_early_Universe.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Fingerprint_of_the_early_Universe.jpg/220px-Fingerprint_of_the_early_Universe.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Fingerprint_of_the_early_Universe.jpg/330px-Fingerprint_of_the_early_Universe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Fingerprint_of_the_early_Universe.jpg/440px-Fingerprint_of_the_early_Universe.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1121" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fingerprint_of_the_early_Universe.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spectrum from quasar HE&#160;0940-1050 after it has travelled through <a href="/wiki/Intergalactic_medium" class="mw-redirect" title="Intergalactic medium">intergalactic medium</a></div></div></div>
<p>Quasars also provide some clues as to the end of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Reionization" title="Reionization">reionization</a>. The oldest known quasars (<a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift"><i>z</i></a>&#160;=&#160;6)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (January 2021)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> display a <a href="/wiki/Gunn%E2%80%93Peterson_trough" title="Gunn–Peterson trough">Gunn–Peterson trough</a> and have absorption regions in front of them indicating that the <a href="/wiki/Intergalactic_medium" class="mw-redirect" title="Intergalactic medium">intergalactic medium</a> at that time was <a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutral_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Neutral gas (page does not exist)">neutral gas</a>. More recent quasars show no absorption region, but rather their spectra contain a spiky area known as the <a href="/wiki/Lyman-alpha_forest" title="Lyman-alpha forest">Lyman-alpha forest</a>; this indicates that the intergalactic medium has undergone reionization into <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>, and that neutral gas exists only in small clouds.
</p><p>The intense production of <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionizing</a> <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation is also significant, as it would provide a mechanism for reionization to occur as galaxies form. Despite this, current theories suggest that quasars were not the primary source of reionization; the primary causes of reionization were probably the earliest generations of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Population_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Population III">Population III</a> stars (possibly 70%), and <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarf galaxies</a> (very early small high-energy galaxies) (possibly 30%).<sup id="cite_ref-popIII_sim_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popIII_sim-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qso_z_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qso_z-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bouwens_LLG_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouwens_LLG-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qso_source1_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qso_source1-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qso_source0_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qso_source0-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qso_source2_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qso_source2-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Quasars show evidence of elements heavier than <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, indicating that galaxies underwent a massive phase of <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a>, creating <a href="/wiki/Population_III_stars" class="mw-redirect" title="Population III stars">population III stars</a> between the time of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> and the first observed quasars. Light from these stars may have been observed in 2005 using <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> although this observation remains to be confirmed.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasar_subtypes">Quasar subtypes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Quasar subtypes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(general)" class="mw-redirect" title="Taxonomy (general)">taxonomy</a> of quasars includes various subtypes representing subsets of the quasar population having distinct properties.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Radio-loud quasars</b> are quasars with powerful <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_jet" title="Astrophysical jet">jets</a> that are strong sources of radio-wavelength emission. These make up about 10% of the overall quasar population.<sup id="cite_ref-peterson1997_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peterson1997-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Radio-quiet quasars</b> are those quasars lacking powerful jets, with relatively weaker radio emission than the radio-loud population. The majority of quasars (about 90%) are radio-quiet.<sup id="cite_ref-peterson1997_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peterson1997-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Broad absorption-line (BAL) quasars</b> are quasars whose spectra exhibit broad absorption lines that are blueshifted relative to the quasar's rest frame, resulting from gas flowing outward from the active nucleus in the direction toward the observer. Broad absorption lines are found in about 10% of quasars, and BAL quasars are usually radio-quiet.<sup id="cite_ref-peterson1997_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peterson1997-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In the rest-frame ultraviolet spectra of BAL quasars, broad absorption lines can be detected from ionized carbon, magnesium, silicon, nitrogen, and other elements.</li>
<li><b>Type 2 (or Type II) quasars</b> are quasars in which the accretion disc and broad emission lines are highly obscured by dense gas and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a>. They are higher-luminosity counterparts of Type 2 Seyfert galaxies.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Red quasars</b> are quasars with optical colors that are redder than normal quasars, thought to be the result of moderate levels of dust <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(astronomy)" title="Extinction (astronomy)">extinction</a> within the quasar host galaxy. Infrared surveys have demonstrated that red quasars make up a substantial fraction of the total quasar population.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/OVV_quasar" title="OVV quasar">Optically violent variable</a> (OVV) quasars</b> are radio-loud quasars in which the jet is directed toward the observer. Relativistic beaming of the jet emission results in strong and rapid variability of the quasar brightness. OVV quasars are also considered to be a type of <a href="/wiki/Blazar" title="Blazar">blazar</a>.</li>
<li><b>Weak emission line quasars</b> are quasars having unusually faint emission lines in the ultraviolet/visible spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_celestial_reference_systems">Role in celestial reference systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Role in celestial reference systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quasar_HE_0109-3518.tiff" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Quasar_HE_0109-3518.tiff/lossy-page1-220px-Quasar_HE_0109-3518.tiff.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Quasar_HE_0109-3518.tiff/lossy-page1-330px-Quasar_HE_0109-3518.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Quasar_HE_0109-3518.tiff/lossy-page1-440px-Quasar_HE_0109-3518.tiff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quasar_HE_0109-3518.tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The energetic radiation of the quasar makes <a href="/wiki/Dark_galaxies" class="mw-redirect" title="Dark galaxies">dark galaxies</a> glow, helping astronomers to understand the obscure early stages of galaxy formation.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Because quasars are extremely distant, bright, and small in apparent size, they are useful reference points in establishing a measurement grid on the sky.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/International_Celestial_Reference_System" class="mw-redirect" title="International Celestial Reference System">International Celestial Reference System</a> (ICRS) is based on hundreds of extra-galactic radio sources, mostly quasars, distributed around the entire sky. Because they are so distant, they are apparently stationary to current technology, yet their positions can be measured with the utmost accuracy by <a href="/wiki/Very-long-baseline_interferometry" title="Very-long-baseline interferometry">very-long-baseline interferometry</a> (VLBI). The positions of most are known to 0.001 <a href="/wiki/Arcsecond" class="mw-redirect" title="Arcsecond">arcsecond</a> or better, which is orders of magnitude more precise than the best optical measurements.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_quasars">Multiple quasars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quasar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Multiple quasars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A grouping of two or more quasars on the sky can result from a chance alignment, where the quasars are not physically associated, from actual physical proximity, or from the effects of gravity bending the light of a single quasar into two or more images by <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a>.
</p><p>When two quasars appear to be very close to each other as seen from Earth (separated by a few <a href="/wiki/Arcsecond" class="mw-redirect" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a> or less), they are commonly referred to as a "double quasar". When the two are also close together in space (i.e. observed to have similar redshifts), they are termed a "quasar pair", or as a "binary quasar" if they are close enough that their host galaxies are likely to be physically interacting.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As quasars are overall rare objects in the universe, the probability of three or more separate quasars being found near the same physical location is very low, and determining whether the system is closely separated physically requires significant observational effort. The first true triple quasar was found in 2007 by observations at the <a href="/wiki/W._M._Keck_Observatory" title="W. M. Keck Observatory">W. M. Keck Observatory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Kea" title="Mauna Kea">Mauna Kea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/LBQS_1429-008" title="LBQS 1429-008">LBQS 1429-008</a> (or QQQ J1432-0106) was first observed in 1989 and at the time was found to be a double quasar. When <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomers</a> discovered the third member, they confirmed that the sources were separate and not the result of gravitational lensing. This triple quasar has a redshift of <i>z</i> = 2.076.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The components are separated by an estimated 30–50&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kiloparsecs" class="mw-redirect" title="Kiloparsecs">kiloparsecs</a> (roughly 97,000–160,000 light-years), which is typical for interacting galaxies.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, the second true triplet of quasars, QQQ&#160;J1519+0627, was found with a redshift <i>z</i> = 1.51, the whole system fitting within a physical separation of 25&#160;kpc (about 80,000 light-years).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first true quadruple quasar system was discovered in 2015 at a redshift <i>z</i>&#160;=&#160;2.0412 and has an overall physical scale of about 200&#160;kpc (roughly 650,000 light-years).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A multiple-image quasar is a quasar whose light undergoes <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a>, resulting in double, triple or quadruple images of the same quasar. The first such gravitational lens to be discovered was the double-imaged quasar <a href="/wiki/Q0957%2B561" class="mw-redirect" title="Q0957+561">Q0957+561</a> (or Twin Quasar) in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
An example of a triply lensed quasar is PG1115+08.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
Several quadruple-image quasars are known, including the <a href="/wiki/Einstein_Cross" title="Einstein Cross">Einstein Cross</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cloverleaf_Quasar" class="mw-redirect" title="Cloverleaf Quasar">Cloverleaf Quasar</a>, with the first such discoveries happening in the mid-1980s.
</p>
